The egg part reminds me of my college days, way back in the 1980's. This was still basically pre-internet, so getting information about things like this was a little more difficult. My roommates and I heard somewhere that it was impossible to cook an egg in the microwave because it would explode - so of course we had to try it. Sure enough, they blew up pretty spectacularly in the microwave. But I figured that this had to be because the egg was being cooked much more rapidly in the microwave (as seen by the fact that the exploded egg fragments were already hard boiled and it hadn't taken all that long to get this result). So I went through about as many eggs as I could afford as a poor student trying to adjust cook power and cook time in order to get a good hard boiled egg from the microwave. On my last attempt I thought I'd done it - no explosion in the microwave, and when I took the eggs out I was able to peel them without incident. Then I bit into one and it blew up in my face.

I think in a lot of these 'Ann got it wrong' videos people misunderstand her point. So many times she's just saying that the 'hack' or suggestion isn't practical. A "hack," by definition should make a process easier, not just be true.  It's not practical to color buttercream to make it darker, only to also make it denser. She's always said it works, she just doesn't recommend it because it basically changes fluffy buttercream into sweetened, colorful butter.  Even if the color change did come from a more efficient distribution of the dye in the emulsion, the fact that the resulting frosting is denser means that either 1) the effect was at least partially caused by reducing the air in the buttercream or 2) the change in texture is a separate, unintended side effect of the hack that limits the usefulness of the frosting.   Even if the buttercream remains a shade darker than the original color after a second whipping, it's not such a significant change (either in this video or in the original creator's video) that you couldn't get it by adding more dye which would be less work for everyone.  It's not practical to eat kale or broccoli gram for gram in the same quantities you would drink milk, fortified orange juice or even eat yogurt.   Although a smoothie could work, it's probably not what most people would call a tasty, stand alone drink. Calcium is also more important to children and teens, so having it in a form they will readily consume is also important. (And post menopausal people, so drink your kale smoothies!)  It's not practical to buy two baking trays of exactly the same material in two different colors when the results aren't significant. That was her point in testing all of those different trays.   It's more important to pay attention to the material and thickness of the pans you have than the color. That's going to make the bigger difference in your baking.  People forget that useability, feasibility and practicality are important features of science, perhaps more so with a YouTube channel than in biochemistry.   Something could be deemed  statistically significant in a laboratory setting, but have no real-world value or application.

Adding water to the potato water pretty much invalidates the experiment.

20:33 I will be frank: I do not care to run the experiment myself, though I stand with you nonetheless. It just makes sense to me from a scientific standpoint. It‚Äôs essentially the same as why tattoos lose their color as you get older: your skin stretches, spreading the color out. When the frosting is aerated, it‚Äôs spread out, and when you blend it all that coloring is compacted, making the color richer. So re-aerating it would just respread the color out, making it lighter again.

I posted this in response to someone on a comment in another video in regard to the whole Ann vs Adriana drama and I wanted to share this here because there is a lot of slander from Adriana's viewers towards Ann regarding the type of buttercream she was testing with, how she was presenting her side and opinion, and even the value of Ann's knowledge and expertise.   Also, before posting this I carefully went through each video to make sure that the types of buttercream, color variations, statements, and what not I have stated as accurately as possible.  In Sugarologies (Adriana) original video, she uses a Swiss buttercream. It is literally stated, in text, that she is using Swiss buttercream. How to Cook That (Ann) also uses a Swiss buttercream in her first video. So this whole ‚ÄúAnn didn‚Äôt use the same/right buttercream‚Äù spiel is just full bs. And people keep harping on that and it is frustrating as hell because people aren‚Äôt paying attention and are thus spreading lies.  Now, for the second video from each. Adriana, in her own video acknowledge that the air is knocked out which was something not disclosed previously and pointed out by Ann. SHE used ‚ÄúAmerican‚Äù buttercream in her response video to debunk Ann with the ‚Äúsame colors‚Äù excuse. Then she proceed to also use Swiss buttercream. Now, to me, the colors on both buttercreams are the same between the two at each respective stage. Ann, in her second video, states she uses a Swiss meringue buttercream. The exact same type of buttercream as before and what Adriana initially said she used. So, again, this whole ‚Äúnot the same buttercream‚Äù is bull and Adriana is being deceptive by stating Ann used a buttercream she did not in fact use.  In the email exchange, you can see that in the attached photo showing Adriana‚Äôs results, the re-whipped is still lighter than the emulsified version. Not as light as the original, but still lighter. So Adriana disproved herself but is unable to or unwilling to acknowledge that because she tried calling wolf.   Adriana called foul because another scientist with a baking knowledge posed questions and pointed out some variables that some people do care about (such as the density and volume of the buttercream). Ann, from her own curiosity, tested adding air back in to see if the color could be retained and wanted to make sure that people had that disclaimer before trying it out and thinking that only the color would change. Clearly Adriana took that as Ann being mean, instead of a scientist peer-reviewing her method, and then proceeded to complain to her viewers about it.  Ann is not wrong to point out that there are variables that may have affected the results. Any good scientist worth their salt would at least do that. You need to consider all possibilities that could have contributed to the studies findings. Maybe the type of butter (European style, grass-fed, etc) or local humidity affected the results, or as Ann suggested, the method of whipping (which it seems that Adriana didn‚Äôt mention in her instruction/methods to Ann? It wasn‚Äôt clear to me).  There are variables that can be controlled and those that cannot. That is why there is a method that of operations must be followed and properly communicated so that it can be replicated and tested by peers in full. Adriana is either 1) being stubborn and not willing to concede on certain points or 2) purposefully sabotaging Ann with her instructions by not including relevant information.  Now, Ann never called Adriana a liar or anything close. Ann stated that she was skeptical, had questions, and even after reaching out and getting answers from Adriana, certain explanations still didn‚Äôt add up to her based on the results she was seeing. Hence, the mentioning of possible variables. Being that they are both scientists, a method of operations must be replicable. Research scientists who publish papers and who then see that someone else published a paper that followed the same method with different results don‚Äôt act the way Adriana did. They go back to their original work, redo the study and compare it to the conflicting results and consider what might have changed if the research scientists still get the same results. Or, they acknowledged they messed up then retract or correct their original publishing.  Again both women are scientists. Just because Adriana has a PhD does not automatically make her more knowledgable or more deserving of respect than Ann. Adriana has an undergrad in Microbiology and Immunology, and a PhD in Biochemistry and Molecular Biology. Ann has an undergrad in Nutrition and Food Science but has several decades, almost as long as Adriana has been alive, of applied science experience. Adriana is a hobbyist where baking is concerned. I‚Äôve read around the comments of many other videos and forums trying to learn more about this process and I‚Äôve seen many people who are microbiologists and who bake for hobby (or professionally after a career change) like Adriana calling bs on the explanation of her method. As someone who has made cakes as a full time job for over a decade as well as studied molecular science, i can say without a doubt that your results with whipping and blending the frosting are very straightforward. imagine the particles are balloons and when you blow them up with air, the rubber (icing particles) expands and becomes almost translucent, winding up a larger pale version of the deflated balloon. That girl is right about one thing, the pigment is more disbursed after blending, but that has nothing to do with the color depth. if anything, it causes the color to homogenize more and you can actually tell the "rewhipped" smear you have is slightly lighter in the end despite being denser than the original volume
For the frosting questions- I think a really good way to see this effect in action outside of the frosting debate is to look at videos of hard candy pulling. It starts super bright and clear, and then as air is added, it becomes lighter and more opaque. Same thing is happening in reverse with the frosting. It starts with air which lightens the color then loses that air. The candy's texture is also changed. There are great videos of this effect all over youtube and many candy makers explain what's happening as they're pulling.

#Expirment I only started watching your debunking videos, but in regards to the food coloring problem. You actually don't need to do an experiment really because this is physics problem that is actually decently well understood. A lot of this has to do with the amount of air being added into the mixture, because the air is going to change the how light is refracted and reflected off the surface of the whipped cream. Whipped cream is a type of foam and is a stable mixture of air suspended within a liquid/solid, and it is those little pockets of air that help reflect more white light back into the eye. So, from a physics point of view, if you could completely and fully re-aerate the whipped cream back to original density, you will get the same amount of reflected light, and the color will appear lighter. ALL foams become opaque and lighter whether you are making whipped cream or hard candies. There is no physical way to do what she is claiming, there is some loss in the system. So it is more likely that she is failing to return the mixture to its original volume, or the shape of the container is affecting how light is entering and exiting the whipped cream and vessel.  Stretching silly putty, bending an eraser, etc. This is the same physics behind why snow is white and while all soap foams turn white regardless of the color the liquid.  Here are my sources that I had to do a fair amount of digging to find https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/agricultural-and-biological-sciences/whipped-cream https://www.mygermantable.com/how-to-make-candy-part-3-aeration/#:~:text=This%20is%20because%20the%20air,this%20from%20beating%20egg%20whites.

The fizzing with soda is technically physical chemistry. It is called surface chemistry, and some chemists find it their least favourite part of a chemistry degree course. It was the only bit I was any good at, as I was always more of a physicist.
on the frosting thing. food coloring often doesn't fully integrate with the more solid components of a buttercream. it can become suspended or encapsulated in itty-bitty bubbles, like an emulsion. it's easy to miss, but if you use particularly dark colors, you can actually see it in american buttercream until the bubbles get small enough that you start seeing them as light instead of unmixed.  if something in the minutia of how she's doing this disperses the coloring more evenly, it would look darker.

From a really simple high school science perspective, I just don't see how it's physically possible for the whipping/mixing/rewhipping process to magically produce a darker color in the same substance. The saturation of color we perceive in any object is simply and unequivocally a case of the pigment density, as in the amount of pigment per given mass and volume. If you have the same amount of pigment but reduce the volume, the pigment density is now higher and the object will appear darker/more saturated. But no matter what you do it, if you have the same amount of pigment in the same amount of material (by whipping the same amount of air back in), the color simply will not change.  The only other argument is that it's not merely an argument of pigment density, but there is some other physical or chemical reaction happening that is affecting the color. I could only imagine this to imply that there is something going on with heat, friction, agitation, or something like that. But none of those effects are being produced in sufficient quantities to change the color of food.  So between this elementary understanding of science, and Ann's experiments, I'm confident that the original hack is fully debunked.

#experiment  I tested the colour thing. I tried both water and oil food colouring and I tried both American and swiss butter cream and as started rewhipping them, I saw the colour drain.   With Swiss bc, I couldn't rewhip to the same volume (maybe due to permanent volume loss of the egg white portion). But as the volume (air) was added, the colour started fading. It seems impossible to add colourless air to mixture and expect the colour not to to be diluted.   Indeed when you whip butter, it becomes paler and paler the more air is incorporated. Now here I did something interesting... I used the immersion blender on the whipped butter and the whipped butter lost some volume and it became yellower again! So the theory that the intense colour is due to emulsification goes out the window. 20:31

I was hoping someone else would say it, but I haven't quite seen it. Spotting legit rescues is difficult, but not impossible: There are a few things to look for.  1. Do they have actual adoption information with a legitimate, physical address? And are they only asking for money or are they also hoping to collect volunteers(especially volunteer vets)? A legit rescue is always short of resources both time and money and a legit rescue will need ample amounts of both to be effective.  2. Are they rescuing very similar-looking animals in similar circumstances? Do the injuries match the claim of how the injuries were reported to have happened? (An example being does it say dog bite and look like a dog bite or say dog bite but look like a cut?) 3. Does the condition of the animal match it‚Äôs environment? I‚Äôve seen white fluffy kittens in mud- if they‚Äôre too dirty or too clean, be suspicious.  4. Look at the place the pets are being kept- does it look like a home or a shelter, or someone‚Äôs back porch or garage? Are they just filling a syringe with some strange liquid and feeding the animal or are they taking it to an actual vet in a sterile environment and assessing the animal properly? A good example is checking a cats eyes, ears, teeth for any conjunctivitis or any signs of infections? Are they properly quarantining the animal before knowing its health status?(Things like FiV, parvo, etc) If we all do our part to keep an eye out and don't hesitate to act, we can keep these videos to a minimum.

A good way to tell if a rescue video might be fake is whether or not they show the after. If there are updates on the animals condition it's a good chance it's real. Another tip is if it seems all the "rescue" videos look like they might be the same animal, or like in Dan's video, you see signs like clue or if things look too deliberately placed to be natural it's is probably fake. Also during the rescue do you get any explanation of how the animal ended up in the situation and how the rescuer found out. If they are a professional, they should have equipment and possibly a team. If they are just a person who happened upon the animal they will probably have to improvise.
It's a good thing to try also - some people like a denser frosting, and don't like a lot of coloring - so it's a great tip to know if that's your style of thing! Knowing options to have in your toolbox of baking, and also just trying stuff out is a great thing I think. See if it works for you. worse comes to worse? You have a very sweet test day that isn't something you repeat but then you know what you think. maybe you like the denser frosting and it works perfectly for you, or you learn you'd prefer to just use a different style and alternate coloring techniques. I'm glad you took a moment to revisit it at least - it's appreciated to see.

I'm no expert in turtles, but I've always heard they have nerves in their shells.  However, turtle guy is wrong about our fingernails.  We do not have nerves in them.  We feel stuff on our nails because of their attachment to the nail bed, not because we sense things through the nail itself. I have tried that buttercream  hack at work with colour mill (which is an oil based colour). After blending it, it‚Äôs too running to ice a cake with it (I also believe this has to do with the amount of extra oil I put in from the colouring). so I put it in the fridge and rewhip it before using it. I never remeasured, but I can say it made a big difference in the colour. I only do this for dark colours though, like black & red, as they take up a lot of colour.
On the frosting experiment I believe I discovered something a few years ago that became a common practice in the cake world until this new method came about. I theorised that the reason the colours weren't intense was because the fat in the butter is repelling the gel colourant, so an emulsion would need to be made. I made the emulsion but taking up to 1/4 of the coloured frosting and softening it in the microwave almost to the point of melting, then allowing that to cool slightly before whipping back into the frosting. This allowed me to get super deep rich red, black and navy colours that are very difficult with buttercream. This works for both American and Swiss/Italian Meringue buttercreams.  I only used it for red, blue and black really as those colours tend to develop more as the frosting sits, but are also develped quicker by heat. Would love to see if that works for you! :)

The potato hack only works if you cube the potato and leave it in the dish, because the blandness of the potato helps to balance the saltiness. It's an old cowboy/voyageur trick for correcting stews made with salt pork. Plus, it added starch and fiber from the skins to give a bit of more complete nutrition.

With colouring butter cream darker. I usually take a small qty of the whipped and ready coloured butter cream (about 2 tablespoons from a full batch) Heat it for a few seconds in the microwave until melted. That darkens the cream. I cool it down before adding it to the rest of the butter cream and mixing well. I repeat this until I achieve the desired colour .

I'm no scientist, but isn't it completely normal for the color to get lighter when there's air in it? Like, the air causes the space in the mass and basically spreads out the pigments, making it look lighter? Like snow looking white because there's individual ice crystals, reflecting the light and making it look white, while it's transparent once you consense it into a bals of solid ice, with no air, so no individual particles. Or a balloon that's being inflated, stretching out what is basically it's original surface mass and the pigmented material, making it wear thin. Even color is part of a physical make up. There is never going to be more of a mass, just because you spread or enhance it, it's just gonna wear thin. There shouldn't be a way of having air in it and be the same color as with less air in it. #experiment I used a Swiss meringue butter cream with 4 drops of food colour which was about 230ml. After blending the texture completely changed, the butter cream became glossy (and extremely runny!) the colour was more intense but it measured only 180ml. After putting it on the fridge and whipping it again it was back to almost the original volume (220ml) but the colour was as pale as before. (and I have photo evidence if anyone doubts my results)  So, surprise, surprise: it was the air after all. Of course air 'diluted' the colour. If you ever made fondant yourself you know that the white colour comes (traditionally) solely from the air you knead into the mixture.   Great video as ever, please keep up the amazing content!

So I think I found the main difference in your technique (#experiment) - she is coloring the finished buttercream, you are coloring the meringue (therefore effectively eliminating her argument about emulsive properties - which already gives you a darked color to start with). Then when she blends the buttercream she introduces the coloring to the water molecules better - resulting in slightly darker color - plus as she knocks the air out, the color change is substantially more visible.   So at this point the experiment already yielded different results (her being more dramatic because of the process she used). So you both are basically correct to some degree. If the food coloring is incorporated after - yes the color will be darker after re-whipping. If one uses the color before - the color will return back to its original state, since the matter of dispersion of color was already taken care of in the meringue part.   The best solution the the matter should be using oil based colors for buttercreams - so there is no need to blend and re-whip (which Adriana kind of shown in her video - contradicting herself - as when she re-whipped the oil coloured frosting it returned to the original light color).   The problem I have with sugarlogy's video is how unnecessarily defensive and rude she comes across. That's not how the first "hack" works - the video shows it wrong and understandably, you don't give it a fair chance. A small thin slice of potato doesn't take the salt away like a magic sponge (and it definitely doesn't need to be square). And it certainly won't work with a whole tablespoon of salt to a saucepan with a cup of liquid like they did in the original tik-tok. If you oversalt your dish a bit the way to get it less salty is to make more - either throw some of it out and add more unsalted ingredients or just add more unsalted ingredients. Meaning that you can add something cheap and saltless inside - like a bunch of potatoes to your Bolognese sauce instead of adding more tomatoes and meat. And obviously more water.

The lifehack by 0:27 actually works good but you need to do some things a bit different! 1. The more salt the more potatoes: Potatoes can absorb the salt to the point where you need to add salt after using this hack. This tiny little piece of potato that is used in the beginning is not enough. I would have used the whole potato, just to make shure it is enough. 2. 15 minutes can be enough, but to make sure the salt is gone just leave the potatoes longer in the soup. Taste tests after 10,15,20,25,... minutes should help finde the right time. After that just take the potatoes out and throw them away.  For the ones interested in why this works: Its a combination of diffusion and osmosis.  The salt in the soup enters the cells of the potatoes. There is a chemical rule where a substance that is dissolved in a liquid tries to diffuse in a way that it is evenly dissolved in the liquid. If you add the potatoes then there is no salt in them. Following the rule the salt tries again to diffuse into everything into the liquid. As the potato can absorb salt, the salt wanders throu the cell wall and stays in the cytoplasm. Again this happens till the concentraion of salt is the same in the soup and the potato. As I said above it helps to have enough potato. As a pice of potato can only absorb a cerntain amount of salt.
Hack with potato works, but it's needed to add bigger piece of potato. I saved a lot of my soups and other dishes with that hack. You need to simmer it in soup or sauce,, and leave potato in after some time, it takes salt from a dish.

On the matter of the food coloring in the frosting, it makes perfect sense both why the color gets darker and lighter correlated to how much air is in the frosting. The air in the frosting is scattering the light that bounces off making it appear lighter. remove the air will make it look darker as the light that bounces off isnt scattered as much. reintroducing the air into the frosting will reintroduce the light scattering effect, but since you're most likely not beating all the air back in it'll apear darker then the original, but it will be lighter then when you blended it.  This is less chemistry and more just physics, and only a rough idea at that. Im sure there are videos here on youtube that better explain it.  Here's one more fun fact, most blues found in nature with a few exceptions aren't actually blue. They appear blue due to how the structure of the object scatters light on a microscopic level.

I don't bake often, but I really enjoy you videos, especially the debunking ones. The talk about icing density and color made me think of another hobby of mine, spinning yarn. There's a well known spinning book that talks about how the way a fiber is prepared and spun, which affects the density of the spun yarn, will affect the perceived saturation of the color. Denser yarns will make the color appear darker, more airy yarns will make the color appear lighter. I'd imagine the same would apply to icing. Just thought it was an interesting and unexpected connection between the two.   I appreciated how you stayed professional, and kept out of YouTube drama. Many thanks for an amazing video!

my mum has always said that potato can reduce the saltiness of a soup, but you have to leave the potato in there! The potato bulks it out while being relatively bland so it dilutes the salt without dramatically changing the flavour of the soup. The idea potato can suck up salt like some sort of reverse osmosis filter I assume is the result of a game of telephone from that
We are using the potato-salt hack in India from years on. It totally works in Indian gravies. What happens is when we add potato to balance the salt, naturally more water is also added to gravy to get right consistency. So the quantity of the gravy is increased, which most of the times works with the salt quantity.

Thanks for making me aware of the fake animal rescue videos - I had no idea! You're doing a great thing raising awareness of this sort of thing, as well as the kinds of dangerous hacks you've talked about before.
Original "hack" was about using an immersion blender on your frosting to increase the color saturation.  Does this work?  Yes.  Ann's "debunking" video pointed out that, while this does work, it results in a very different texture that isn't usually associated with frosting.  Is that true?  Yes.  Original "hack" content creator argues that rewhipping the frosting will regain the "frosting" texture while retaining the color saturation gain.  Is this true?  Well...not really.  Rewhipping the frosting can (potentially, if done correctly) regain the "frosting" texture, but the color of the frosting then desaturates as well.  From what I've seen in the videos, and my own experiment in my kitchen, the "rewhipped" frosting is slightly more saturated than the original frosting but has slightly less volume as well, and I wouldn't say there's a significant difference in saturation..  Original "hack" content creator goes on to argue about the mechanisms which are causing the increased color saturation and makes another video pointing to perceived errors from Ann's video.  Original "hack" content creator's channel gets a significant boost in subscribers and engagement from the "drama" between the two channels.  Original "hack" content creator goes on to posting several comments below her video further challenging Ann's findings and methods, fanning the flames of drama and further boosting her channel's engagement.  People watching both channels take sides and comment, further increasing engagement for both channels.  Honestly, I can't find a fault in the process for either channel.  The fault I do see is in the original content creator's seemingly intentional "stirring of the pot".  It seems obvious to me that the original content creator saw one of her videos on another channel with nearly 5 million subscribers and saw an opportunity to boost her channel.  I can't blame her for doing that, but I'm not thrilled with how she chose to take advantage of the situation.  Many of her statements seems very disingenuous and she seems to be intentionally misrepresenting her findings for the purpose of increasing the drama.    In her video, she shows several samples and says the rewhipped samples retained their higher saturation, but they seem almost identical (they're usually very slightly darker).  In another portion, she shows Swiss made butter cream and the rewhipped version is significantly more saturated than the initial sample, but the video clearly shows a significant decrease in volume between the initial frosting and the rewhipped frosting.  She makes a big point about weighing the frosting, but the weight is irrelevant because nothing is being added and nothing is being removed (except air), then says she measured the volume and found significantly "less than in the debunking video", but she doesn't show the original volume.    Ann isn't always right, and her methods aren't always correct, but I've never watched anything she's done and thought she was intentionally misrepresenting her findings.

The whipped frosting color is simple science, whipping it adds air and thus brightens the color, it's impossible to avoid that. The only way to make it darker and keep it airy is to add more coloring.
Adding volume to frosting without adding additional coloring will make it less vibrant.  Think about it.  You're adding air.  Air doesn't have a color.  So, when you're adding air to your frosting, the color will diminish roughly by the same percentage of air (by volume) you're adding.  Say you have 100ml of frosting and whip it into 150ml of frosting.  The color will lose roughly 50% of its vibrancy.  This isn't even science!  It's simple math!

It is more saturated if you blend it because the color particles are closer together, because you blended out some volume, the color doesn't have to spread that much across the whole volume. I'm no expert, BUT... I do bake cookies using a 1930s recipe that uses butter and sugars creamed together with a fork. What is the 1930s housewife way of knowing when they're ready? By seeing the color and texture change to light and fluffy. Overdo it and it turns dark again and collapses. Why is that related to the frosting drama? Because creamed butter and sugars make the cookies fluffier. When I've overbeaten the mixture, my cookies have turned out much flatter. The thing that makes the mixture turn light is the same thing that makes my cookies fluffy, and that must be air. At least that's how it looks to my simple mind. On animal rescues:  A good general set of guidelines, in my opinion, as someone involved in welfare. 1. Is the source of the content a WELL-KNOWN and welfare* based organization locally, nationally, or internationally? 2. Does the title use a to-the-point summarization instead of emotionally focused clickbait terms? 3. If you're unfamiliar with the source; is it an ACTUAL welfare-based organization? A zoological organization? A veterinary clinic? What does the source's "about" show, and do they have a significant presence off of social media? Google is always your friend!  *Welfare is preferred over "animal rights" organizations for a long list of reasons, but that's a very separate topic.

When I watched the other lady's responding frosting video something about her logic bothered me. And this video actually addressed and answered that question indirectly. She acknowledged that there was air loss, but ignored that factor as a possible explanation of the color change. She still claimed her theory on why the color changed was the right explanation and that the air loss had no effect on the color.  She argued that the reason that blending it worked is because it forced the small droplets of water soluble dye to contact and mix with the larger droplets of water in the emulsion. The idea being that the butter fat prevented the dye from dissolving in the other water and forced it to stay in concentrated small droplets and that this resulted in a less intense color.  Even accepting the theory that small intensely colored droplets appear less saturated to the eye than larger less intensely colored droplets (which I'm unsure of, but lets say is true) that can't be a factor in the swiss meringue made in this video because the dye was added directly to the eggs, which is the water based portion of a swiss meringue buttercream. This ensures that the dye was not prevented from mixing with all of the water based potion of the emulsion.  Which also means that the emulsion shown in this video contains 3 things only: 1 butterfat;  2 a solution of sugar, water, egg white and dye; and 3 air.  Not 4 things, as she claimed in her video:  1 butterfat;  2 a solution of sugar, water, and eggwhite; 3 dye; and  4 air.  If her theory was correct, then the color of the swiss meringue made in this video would not intensify with blending. The fact that it does proves that incompletely dissolved dye is not a factor.  The only thing that could cause the loss of volume is the loss of the air. Therefore the fact that blending it changes the color in both this video's swiss meringue buttercream (with its dye added directly to the eggs) and the frosting she made in her video (with dye added at an undetermined stage) means that the only remaining explanation is that the loss of the air intensifies the color.

Regarding the color:   Foam is refracting light (that's why foam looks white) and it's decreasing the color-to-volume ratio.  Getting rid of the air will make it darker and adding in air bubbles will make it lighter.
I love these videos, but the one about a potato NOT making food less salty is simply not true. The potato absorbs the water and takes salt with it. Why else would you salt water to season potatoes if you're cooking them to eat? Same thing with pasta. Removing salt from the solution isn't going to necessarily reduce the concentration, but if you then top the pot off with fresh water (or w/e other ingredients for the dish) the concentration will be less. Whether that's a meaningful difference would depend on the person tasting it, but still.

I think the potato thing is a case of life hack telephone taking an idea that sorta works and making it into a 'flashier' hack that does not. Putting a potato in an over-salted dish does help... but not if you take it out! The problem with salty food is too much salt not enough food. So if you just add more food (especially something dense and absorbent like potato or rice), you even out the balance a little. Many times when I've had my food come out too salty, I just spread it over a generous amount of mashed potato or cooked rice and eat it like that. Big improvement. THAT is how a potato helps over salted food- not through cooking hack absorbancy magic!

For those curious about the colored frosting science..... when you lose air volume, the colored 'bits' that give the frosting its color grow closer together, denser.  This gives the effect of making it appear darker, more intense.  The second you add air volume back into the mixture, the colored 'bits' are going to become more spread out, less dense.  This results in a less intense color.

Dave!
I am with Ann regarding the frosting experiment that the coloring is most likely related to the airiness or texture of the mixture. The coloring is mostly controlled by the light absorption of the mixture based on the coloring chemicals that are added it therefore can relatively easily be described by Lambert-Beers law. The absorption is linearily proportional to the molar absorption coefficient which doesnt chang since its material specifc, the concentration of absorbing material and the length of the light path through said material. Given the experiment by making it more fluffly you are reducing the concentration of absorbant which causes the absorption to decrease and therefore the coloring to become lighter. The only way to keep a darker coloring while also decreasing the concentration would be using a longer lightpath. Now the length of the lighpath is a bit more complicated here because we are not dealing with transmission through a relatively transparent medium but instead with absorption by partial reflection and scatteringe i.e. the penetration and reflection depth inside the material matters. I guess depending on what frosting you exactly have it could be possible that you increase the penetration depth and scattering of the light (i.e. the light path) more than you decrease the concentration of absorbant by whipping it up but my guess would be that this would require relatively specific conditions and it is much more likely that the overall absorption will go down again when whipping the frosting up.  In any case for the color to be different the texture has to be different even if it might not be that perceptible to you since either the light path or the concentration (probably both) need to change for their to be a darker coloring percieved.

If you're using smaller batches for testing the frosting color, the problem you'll run into is the precision of your volume measurements. Say your measuring cup allows you to estimate +/- 20 mL. Say the re-whipping process causes a loss of 10% of volume. The cup won't allow you to tell if you've lost 10% of a 100mL volume. Larger quantities will help mitigate that precision limitation, as well was reduce the percent error that would occur from minor losses of frosting on the bakeware (though it sounds like you were also doing a good job of accounting for that with mass measurements).

The ginger milk reminds me of making a pineapple dessert set with gelatin (like jelly or curd for example). Pineapple has an enzyme (bromelain) that breaks down proteins so using fresh pineapple (or lightly treated) would never set, whereas cooking it long enough to deactivate the enzyme or using canned pineapple (which is heat treated), didn't incur in any problems when setting.  Regarding the buttercream, although the blending premise of dispersing the droplets more evenly is true, the volume/air is the determining factor. For experiments like this there could be many other variables that would need to be equalized, like atmosphere, ingredients, methods, time, etc.   How fun would it be to have a lab dedicated to this stuff? "Let's run this buttercream through a spectrophotometer and measure the intensity" Some billionaire make it happen and hit me up, have plenty of ideas and hypotheses. LOL

maybe check the temperatures of the buttercream at each step? i remember reading something that bravetart wrote about how you want to add cool (around ~65 f [sorry, i'm american]) butter to buttercream and work towards a final temp in the low to mid 70s, because butter over 70 f won't hold air as well.  so i'm thinking if you started with buttercream at that working temp (which is where it'll naturally land after adding cool butter to the meringue) and then immersion blended it, and THEN immediately re-whipped you may just not see the same aeration due to the butter temperature being too high to be able to aerate properly.  given that adriana mentions using an ice bath to get the volume back--which would cool the butter in the buttercream a bit, i'm thinking to a temperature where it is able to hold air better). i've also read some other 'hacks' to deepen their buttercream color that are temperature based: one where you melt it all in the microwave, cool it, and rewhip; and another person online said they just chuck the whole bowl in the freezer for a little bit to darken their buttercream. so it is also possible that the difference she's seeing is maybe JUST based on the cooler temp (one might have to include a temperature control to really know, idk).   other than temp it may also be down to a difference in type of butter (i know i've read theres a difference in american vs european butter, not sure about butter in aus), type of food coloring (was yours gel or liquid? not sure if it was mentioned) or something else we aren't considering

With salt what really can happen is if you salt something like a stew too early the sodium can bind to proteins in the food as it cooks.  It will become less salty tasting over time but winds up having just as much dietary sodium.  The problem is that most people then add additional salt to taste so in the end the stewed version has extra sodium in it.  (There was an America's Test Kitchen episode about this several years ago where they tested the idea fairly rigorously.)

A way to get the frosting back to volume without lightening the colour is to introduce bigger bubbles. Those have thicker walls, so there's more coloured material to reflect the light before it hits air.

I think the huge difference in results is that you adding the colour BEFORE the butter and she didn't. The stick blender is designed the slightly break the emulsion to allow it to incorporate into the mixture more. No point to doing that if you added the food colour before you formed the emulsion in the first place.  Done the stick blender hack myself multiple times with Swiss Merigue Buttercream. Similar results to the microwave method but MUCH easier to do. I've only done it with small quanitities and a hand mixer.  Yes, the frosting is a little thicker but my husband couldn't tell the difference in a blind taste test. I can't taste the difference either, but even if I could, there's only a thin layer of it on the outside most of the time anyways, since the inside and the crumb coat I just do with uncoloured buttercream. Most of the taste comes from there.  Additionally, you sometimes cannot get the colours that you need with just gel colouring. I think this hack is for those instances. I know before I knew this hack I would get very frustrated trying to get rich or dark colours and kept adding more food colouring with no change. Use a stick blender and a quick rewhip and my problem is solved üëç game changer!

Not that this is important but didn‚Äôt you make Italian meringue buttercream and not Swiss meringue buttercream? Hi Anne, It looks like there's a major technique difference here between your method and Adrianna's.  You're adding dye to your meringue BEFORE the butter, whereas Adrianna makes a plain white buttercream, portions it, and adds dye AFTER the butter is already incorporated.  Using water-based dyes means the butter is interfereing with the accessibility of the water-based component of the buttercream (the egg white). Blending it further just allows the dye to access the water in the meringue better, giving a more homogeneous result. I agree that re-whipping is not effective, but the point at which you add the dye is INTEGRAL to how dye is distributed, and therefore affects the reflectance and perceived chroma of the buttercream.
Hi Anne, It looks like there's a major technique difference here between your method and Adrianna's. Animal rescue vids: I'ma veterinarian (cat/dog) that also helps out with a wildlife rehab at times. I would say cat/dog probably harder to tell; but for wildlife I do have some guidelines on if it's legit!  1) is it shared by an organization working with animal rescue? While individuals can definitely do good, and there are many independent licensed wildlife rehabbers, in general it's most trustworthy if it's coming for an organization licensed for animal rescue/rehabilitation. For example I never post anything personally about the wildlife work I do, I just share posts from the organization.  2) what precautions are being used? In general people who are doing good for the sake of the animal are probably wearing gloves - either latex exam gloves or thick leather gloves depending on the species. Animals should be being held and restrained properly; this is difficult to have a general guideline for but looking on google images of people holding birds of prey or thinking about how you've seen your dog/cat be held at the vet. This is also a good one for the cat/dog rescue videos 3) 9/10 if a medical procedure or something similar is being shown on wildlife- the animal should be sedated/anesthetized for wildlife. There are rare exceptions but the turtles being awake while they scraped things off the shell - not okay 4) Is what's being described make sense. E.g. those turtles like he was saying with seeing the glue and them being freshwater turtles. Does it make sense that a baby raccoon should be sitting on someone's couch eating ritz crackers. Again this is harder but taking a few seconds to think about.

#Experiment: Whipped three batches of Anne's swiss meringue buttercream recipe with red, blue, and yellow food gel coloring, scaled down to 1 egg white each batch, using Wilton branded gel.  After the immersion blender step, I took it to the next level and whipped it until the cream basically self-destructed (don't overwhip your meringue recipes) and never got back the same 'darker' color as the immersion blender 'made' it -- it's simple to see why. When you add air bubbles to the mixture the first time around you are lightening the mixture. When you use an immersion blender you are taking the air out of it and the mixture appears darker. By then your mixture of sugar, eggs, and cream is already past its peak stability -- if you agitate it any further the structure will break. You see the same "darkening color" when you make macarons -- especially during the macaronage stage. When you macaronage the air out of the mixture it'll appear darker because there's less air. Of course, the color can change again after it's baked, but that's a whole other beast.  TL;DR: listen to Anne, the food science specialist -- and not a random tiktok video. Also don't microwave eggs.

I'm not a science bird, but stretching something out with air, like the whip cream, it'll make the color particles stretch out and make it lighter in color. It's no different than adding color to a bowl of water and then adding more water to that bowl... It'll get lighter. Boil the water and as the water vaporizes, it'll get darker in color(unless the color also vaporizes, which I doubt) Not convinced? Try blowing air into a balloon, then deflating it, then adding that air back into the balloon... its literally the same experiment as with the whip cream except way easier and less tasty...
Ann you don't have to be so nice. Adriana is simply wrong. The color saturation is a basic function of concentration. If you aren't adding more coloring reducing the volume of icing will concentrate, and therefore, darken the final product. Increasing the volume by adding air will conversely lighten the icing.

Speaking just from a theoretical perspective, X volume of food dye will be needed to turn Y volume of a specific frosting a certain color -- that's just how dyes work. The volume ratio is what is important, as has been demonstrated very well by Ann. IF, somehow, you could use less of the same dye to achieve the same color with the same volume of the same frosting, that wouldn't simply be a hack, it would be a miracle of science!

*TLDR* For the *animal* *rescue* videos there is definitely an issue with differentiating what is real and what is fake. I think a good starting point would be for people watching these videos to be extra vigilant in looking for signs that will make it easier to tell them apart. For example legitimate animal rescue videos will generally have professionals who do this as a job and will get called out by the public, in these videos the rescue teams will normally have uniforms and specific equipment that is used for specific rescues and animals, they should have no issue showing licenses, they will normally have a website not a just a social media account and phone numbers to the region they work. Fake rescues may be difficult to spot but a few things to look out for if you have suspicion somethings not right. - Look at when the account of the person uploading started, more recent account don‚Äôt necessarily mean they are fake but it could be a sign they have created new accounts having been banned from others in the past. - look at other videos on the channel to see what they previously uploaded, if they started out as a makeup blog channel or gaming channel and then all of a sudden they show an animal rescue that got better views and then they begin uploading videos of more animal rescues then chances are they are trend jumping for views and even though there is a high number of animals that need rescued it is very unlikely that a random person located in a specific area will just happen to come across an animal in danger on a weekly or even monthly bases, even if they go out with the intention to try and find one themselves it would be very hard to do. - There are some legitimate channels that may have lots of rescues that don‚Äôt look like they are charity based or professionally licensed but rather they have developed their own reputation and volunteer to rescue based on a set of skills they have learned over years looking after animals. These people will be able to make videos that are genuine because they have been doing it in their local area probably before YouTube was a thing, these channels are easy to spot as they are able to demonstrate and show a lot of in depth knowledge and skill around caring for animals and for the most part are normally centred around the rescue and care of a specific species rather than a broad range of different animals. So i don‚Äôt want to spam promote certain channels but they will generally be content creators who foster cats or dogs, or creators that show how to look after or keep fish or birds or will have a background in niche areas like marine biology or zoology. A lot of these channels will have high numbers of videos showing sick, rescued  or abandoned animals because they are already established in that field and are the first point of contact to other professionals and veterinarians within that area, so they are not necessarily coming across these animals themselves they are specifically called upon. - if you do see a one off rescue uploaded on YouTube it may be legitimate. If the person is taking it upon themselves to do the rescue and record it, they may have very good intentions and just want to show others that they are doing a really good deed but realistically it‚Äôs probably best that they call an animal rescue centre or charity first. There will always be times where time may not allow that, in which case if you are gonna attempt a rescue yourself just make sure you are not putting yourself or others in danger, look at the risks involved first, and think about any aftermath consequences for example an adult cat stuck at the side of the road you might be able to pull over and lift it but will you be able to drive away with it in the car, it is scared and doesn‚Äôt know what‚Äôs happening it might scrab or bite but worse case scenario it might hide under the brake pedal or step on the accelerator so just be careful for things like that. - The only other things to do if you suspect a fake rescue is ask the creator questions, like what happened to the animal after? Did they call professionals? Can they show an update or where the animal is being cared for or recovering? If they show multiple rescues ask them how they know where to find these animals in danger. Most people should have no issues in answering questions about the well being of an animal since that‚Äôs suppose to be their whole deal, so if they get quite defensive or just never answer anyone‚Äôs questions then I‚Äôd see that as a major red flag.

"what is this, some kind of bad gatorade" actually, YES! Gatorade is basically fruit juice (or artificial fruit juice) plus salt in water. So like. citric acid plus sugar plus salt is basically gatorade. Vinegar is probably less pleasant an acid than citric acid but same idea.  the solution to pollution is dilution works for salt as well, that's where the potato hack comes from. if you are making a stew and the stew is too salty, adding extra unsalted ingredients to the stew will help the taste. If you can cook it long enough to cook potatoes, you may as well add extra of all the non-salt ingredients and freeze the rest of the batch. My solution to the darker frosting problem would be to layer frosting. Do a base layer of the first one for the perfect texture, then a thin layer of the darker one on top of it for the coloring, depending on the icing ratio majority of the icing texture would be the better one, while externally the color you would see is the darker one.  And for detailing you could use the original very airy one in a piping bag.  The end result would be you could  make a big batch of icing, and split it in 3 to save time. one for base layer(original) one for external area(darker) one for icing layers between cake layers and piping decorations(original)

Turtle owner here- Identifying fake animal rescue videos is about the same as noticing any other sort of fake content. It's easy to spot if you know the basic signs. I've never heard of or seen a real turtle rescue video, aside from people helping out a nest of turtle eggs from danger (e.g. when Snake Discovery incubated eggs from a destroyed turtle nest) or a channel giving advice on how to help turtles cross roads. If a wild turtle does have anything on their shell, normally it's just some algae, you're not supposed to do anything with a wild turtle unless you need to take them to a vet.  Easiest sign of a fake rescue video is when the animal is covered in something that the video makers could have obviously or easily put on the animal on purpose. The examples in the video like gluing rocks to a turtle shell or putting tar on dogs are good examples. Coverage that deliberately and evenly placed wouldn't happen naturally to a turtle or dog. Good or real turtle videos are put out by reputable sources like well-known reptile channels or wildlife rescue organizations, not channels like in the video examples.
The potato hack definitely works in curries and such. It is a hack my grandmother taught me and it saved my life so many times. üëç I would have used more potato and let it simmer for longer, it probably works for curry and stew but not water.
I was always disdainful of fake car and computer restoration videos, but fake animal rescue videos are just disgusting.
EXPERIMENT:  I did the colour hack weeks ago with results very similar to Ann's. I used an American buttercream, which does not fluff up as much as an Italian buttercream. That does make a difference.   I also divided my frosting into smaller batches when I went to colour it so the volume of gel colour to frosting ratio is a little higher per batch than if it out be if I had coloured one singular large batch.  Yes, using the emulsion blender does intensify the colour. That is undeniable. But I did also lose a fair amount in volume. When I tried to whip it up again, I regained some volume but lost some colour intensity as well. It was still fluffier than just basic butter but not as fluffy as it could have been. Since American buttercream isn't as fluffy as Italian or other meringue-based frostings, the colour tends to be a bit darker to begin with so you don't need to use the emulsion blender for as long thereby retaining more of your volume.  I used this with Russian piping tips for some simple flowery cupcakes and It worked well but I would not use this for frosting a larger cake or for doing huge, intricate designs. The density of the buttercream makes it unappetizing in larger amounts.

Everything yes! Except the "Swiss Meringue" Buttercream. That's not it. This is Italian Meringue Buttercream. Swiss Meringue has the same ingredients but uses a different method. You don't cook a sugar syrup, you dissolve the sugar in the egg whites over a double boiler until all the sugar crystals are dissolved and then you whisk it up.  Italian Meringue uses the cooked syrup üòä

I'm of the belief that no, no chemical change is happening. The airiness is going to effect how light reflects off of it and will cause a lighter/darker tone. #experiment - Chemist weighing in on the frosting. It is all about concentration, which is the amount of color divided by the volume of frosting. The reason it gets darker when the air is knocked out is because the volume of the frosting went down, making the color MORE concentrated. This means there is less space between each color molecule, making it appear darker. You can do this with water. Put the same amount of color into different volumes of water and you will see that the glass with the less water will appear darker. Rewhip the frosting, increase its volume, and it WILL get LIGHTER because you are increasing the volume and the space between color molecules. Ann's rewhipped was slightly darker, but it was also more concetrated than her initial meringue. Ann explained this concept with the salt experiment. If you put the same amount into the same volume, but heat one, vaporizing the water, the solution becomes MORE concentrated because the volume is less. Here is some math: 2/700 vs 2/400 vs 2/600. Put each fraction into your calculator and you will see 2/400 has the largest decimal and therefore is more concentrated.

I have an idea: It seems like she is using dry pigment and you are using liquid pigment. The dry pigment can form bubbles in suspensions. The blender breaks the bubbles and the pigment gets released from the bubbles. So it stays a darker color even after whipping again, because the pigments are not in bubbles but distributed more evenly.

Quick little tip on how to actually fix an overly salted meal/drink/sauce/etc: Get rid of about half of it (dispose of it in whatever way is appropriate for what you're making) then refill it with the same ingredients you were using to begin with. For example, In the water experiment Ann showed, just pour half (ish) the water down the sink, and fill in another 250 ml of water. Or if it's easier, get rid of the whole thing and start a new one from scratch.

#Experiment: This is like how a balloon's color lightens when you blow it up, then darkens again when you let the air back out. The color in the frosting is effected by the air in it because the air bubbles stretch the frosting so that the color particles are farther apart, thus lighter. If you take the air out, the bubbles shrink, and the color particles are closer together, so then the color is more concentrated. If you whip the air back in, the bubbles increase the distance between the particles again, so the frosting lightens up again. Maybe not as light as you started with because it's really difficult to get as much air in as you took out. It might appear darker than when you started, but not much.
As for the frosting, I'm curious if her color darkening technique is more noticeable on American-style buttercreams than meringue-based. Her explanation has to do (if I remember correctly, it's been a while since I watched it) with accessing water in the butter to give the water-soluble color more to mix with. Egg whites adding a higher proportion of water to begin with makes them accept coloring more easily without the need to knock nearly as much air out.  Regardless, in practice when rewhipping, I don't see any reason you would need to whip it to the exact volume it had before -- as long as it's a smoothable & appetizing consistency, some amount of volume loss may be an acceptable trade-off. I know I have certain food colorings that can quickly start to taste bad and I would rather have a cake with slightly denser frosting than risk making the entire thing inedible.  I have a batch of green macarons I'm currently making, I may try it on the filling just to see what happens.

I believe you made an Italian meringue buttercream not a Swiss.  A Swiss meringue buttercream is made by whisking egg whites and sugar over a double broiler until hot and then whipping until stiff peaks.  Italian meringue is pouring the hot syrup into already whipped eggs whites.  Don‚Äôt want to confuse anyone. You may have just misspoken.  I love watching your videos! Especially the debunking ones!

The idea of adding a potato to over-salted soups and such is very very old.  TV chefs used to speak of that in the 1970's or maybe earlier.
As a cake decorator for a decade plus you have to use more color gel to get a darker color. Adding air always has taken away the darker color we would just have to add more of the gel to make the color back darker again. But also, it depends on what type of coloring you use. Different food coloring, such as gel or airbrush color is going to get you a different color to begin with. If I were making a say blue if I were to use the gel color, which is more concentrated, I would actually be using less than if I were to you saying airbrush, which is less concentrated more watery. I would have to use more of the airbrush. It‚Äôs what we always do if we want a small, pastel color, rather than using the more concentrated green, I would use just a tiny bit of the airbrush, which would give me a great pastel color.

There's no way the color is going to stay dark in the frosting, it's the air that lightens the color. You can see something similar in hard candy making videos- they'll take hot sugar that's a medium beige color from cooking, and can turn it paper white just by adding enough air. It's completely illogical from any standpoint. The more volume there is, the thinner the dye will be spread, and the lighter the color.

For the frosting hack...I've used a modified American buttercream for years. When it's whipped, there is a limit to the intensity of color I get no matter how much coloring I add. But if I let that frosting sit for at least 24 hours (or frost a cake, then let the cake sit), the color will darken significantly. If I then rewhip the frosting, it will lighten right back up. But again, if I let it sit, back to dark it goes. So it may be an issue of how long the frosting sits as well as the volume. I can for sure confirm that the whipping makes an immediate difference in the color, even in a frosting without eggs. One more thing that might be important: this effect happens with my chocolate buttercream, too. No coloring, just cocoa. #experiment

If your food is slightly overly salty you can add 2 potatoes not just a slice and cook it until the pototoes are soft. Then potatoes will take away some of the saltiness but you other food will probably be over cooked.

Have you ever tried making an egg puff? You whisk the eggs a bit, put either butter or cream cheese in the bowl, and then microwave for 10s, whisk, 20s, whisk, and then let it go until it puffs a bit or doesn't show liquid. The butter one cooks faster and comes out flatter. It tastes just like scrambled eggs. It's usually done in less than a minute. The cream cheese one ends up fluffy with the cream cheese in the middle. You can add salt and pepper to make them savory, or add syrup to make them sweet (they are sometimes referred to as egg pancakes). Super yummy. You do have to pay attention to it while it's in the microwave though so it doesn't overcook or explode. It's recommended that you use a paper bowl as it sticks horribly to the sides of the dish. Also, the cream cheese one may let out one little pop when you stick your fork into it, but I've never had it do more than that in all of the years that I've been making them. Note: I usually put a paper towel over top to prevent any issues in and out of the microwave. I don't usually have any though. That one main pop is mostly just scary sounding.

For the frosting: air is really good at scattering all wavelengths of visible light. When it's incorporated into a substance, that means any pigments will be washed out. This is compounded by the fact that you're also diluting the pigment. What sugarologie is saying is physically Impossible without adding more pigment. Even then, because of the scattering, the pigment won't be as intense.

Ok, so I only got a bit of the Sugarologie vs How to Cook That bustup - but I'm sure the difference was that Adriana microwaved her frosting for a few seconds and then whipped it back up to retain the colour. She didn't use a blender as far as I remember. When you melt the frosting, the colour molucules get absorbed into the fat molucules, rather than just sit suspended between them - so please Ann, what you need to do is follow her instructions exactly. Make the frosting (whichever type she made), melt it for whichever amount of time she melted it and then rewhip it. Don't use a blender, as that only acts on the air content, you are right - you need to apply heat first to maintain the colour depth.
Well the potato hack actually works and I also got it from granny. It's pretty common here in Germany and I used it several times. When you over salted soup or sauce you put a whole peeled potato into it and let it cook together, not just a slice of potato. It has never failed me thus far. I don't know if the potato actually absorbs the salt or if the starch does something to the flavour or binds some salty water making our taste buds less likely to taste Salt or whatever. Forgive me if any of my ignorance of physics, it's been some time so feel free to correct me, but Adreanna's results seem to be defying the conservation of mass. Adding air increases volume. You should not be able maintain the density of pigmentation (hence the shade of color) over a larger volume. Hence why the initial hand blending experiment resulted in a deeper shade, due to higher pigment density over a reduced volume.  I call shenanigans...
The "2 ingredient mousse" is just called "Spanish-style chocolate" (water, pure cocoa powder, a bit of salt and cinnamon optional). The French-style chocolate is made with milk.

My first suspicion with the buttercream would actually be the atmospheric properties of the kitchen. Not only would something like the temperature affect the icing itself, especially less stable types than Swiss buttercream, but edible dyes are normally so brightly coloured because of chemical groups called chromophores. Chromophores need to be optically active (to change the white light that hits them into the coloured light we see), and that oftens makes them at least a little bit chemically reactive too. So differences in temperature, altitude, ventilation and of course the exact dye composition used could lead to teeny tiny transformations in the dye itself from reactions with oxygen in the air, especially since it's standing for so long and getting so much energetic whipping.  It'd be interesting to explore further, but also it seems like, for most bakers, it wouldn't be worth the trouble of whipping, blending and rewhipping to get a more intense colour when you could just add another drop or two, or use a more intense dye. I can't imagine a commercial kitchen ever doing it this way, at least!

The potato "hack" is something I actually know from my grandma, but they got it wrong. Potatoes require a fairly big amount of salt, so you just add potatoes to the dish, period. Since you're cooking them together with your stew you can save some salt (that used to be expensive in the olden days) and control the final taste by changing proportions of salt to everything else in one pot. But no, potatoes don't magically absorb salt and make it disappear, they just go well with salty sauces :P. Ann's method with simply increasing the balance between sweet, sour and salty to cheat your senses is much better.

In my recent experience making mousse, hand mixers are way more aggressive at whipping in air than the stand mixer I had used before. My money is that *that's* the technical difference between your and Sugarologie's re-whipping, if any; the RPMs and size of the mixer blades would have to be comparable just like the other factors.  I know that homogeneity can have a dramatic effect on optical properties and this should be no different for frosting. On the other hand, unless we're certain the same mass of air has been re-added we can't say the frosting has been re-whipped at all.   Your volume measurement is a much wiser choice than mass in this case because the 100mL of air you lose is only ~0.15g, an amount that could easily be hidden by what's lost to tools/transfers. If that volume can't be regained by whipping then it's impossible to say that the color change is kept, so the onus is on Sugarologie/the viewer experimenters to make sure they are able to get close to the original volume after mixing before they evaluate the color. Notably, despite Sugarologie's otherwise meticulous response in her video she's clearly not monitoring volume (and density by extension), the frosting is in an ordinary bowl and mass-measured at the point of adding coloring but before any mixing has occurred; this is a flaw.

As a baker and a chemist myself, I have tried this a few times in the kitchen in the past with the colour frosting. Incorporating air into frosting does make the colour lighter, and changing the volume of frosting you start with does not change this fact.  When they claim rewhipping frosting keeps the dark colour, it can be a few reasons they may have observed this.  - error of repeatable technique, aka they didn‚Äôt whip it again as long, the evenness of whipping.. etc  -the change of the temperature of frosting whipping after whipping   -rewhipping may after the blending may vary in time compare to originally whipping it on the first step.

Unless the food dye is reacting with the air or frosting themselves (& becoming darker though such a process) then whipping it up should *always* make it lighter - you're taking the same amount of food dye & dispersing it through a larger volume of something that is itself lightly coloured (butter/meringue/cream cheese etc) - this necessarily means thinning it out & a lighter overall appearance.

Anyone who's made candy, or seen it made, knows that working air into a sugar emulsion makes confections lighter. Her explanation also doesn't address why powder, water-based, and oil-based colors all become lighter in whipped frosting and darker when air is knocked out.
Hi Anne,   in regards to the colours and frosting,  from years of baking experience and experimenting with different products,  I'd have to agree with your results from the tests.  If you can re-whip frosting back to its original volume and have not added any extra colouring,   there should be the same ration of amount of colouring to volume of frosting and will be the lighter colour again.  From my findings it will only be more vibrant once blended, as with a lower volume, the concentration of colouring with the reduced volume is a lot higher which gives it its more vibrant appearance.  Its similar to how the foam and fizz on a carbonated drink (like the mentos/coke reaction in the video), a small amount is spread across a larger volume due to air bubbles.

There's a legit origin to that potato trick. It doesn't magically soak up the salt though, you just dilute your food with it: effectively making more food to counteract the extra salt. Doesn't work in every recipes, and also the potato needs to be cut smaller, or mashed in some cases. Works on a lot of thicker soups and goulash for example, where even if you taste the potato, it's appropriate and fitting. Porbably won't work on salted caramel though..
I wonder if there's some kind of light scattering going on, and maybe the tints are relative simply due to the frequency of the light reflecting from the particles of the colorant? For example, I have fun playing with candy making. When the color is first introduced, it is very intense, but as the candy is folded and mixed, more air is trapped in the mixture. The color simply does diffuse, because there are more points of diffraction in the mixture after it is pulled and folded than there are before. Frosting is relatively simple to tint, you just have to use enough. Is everyone using the same color, and the same manufacturer of the color? What about the materials? Different dyes will have different properties, and different mixes of frosting will have variables as well, as far as composition, or quality. I'm not saying that anyone is right or wrong, I'm asking, can we rule the dye composition and light frequency variables of the shade out? It seems to me that  if you're reducing the surface area that the particles of tint are diffracting through in the base material, the color will be more vibrant. As more space is made between molecules of the base material, the color will diffuse, it has greater distances to travel and will be affected by the white light traveling the same pathways. The light that travels through the increased space between individual molecules of base material will be more available than there would be if it were compressed. The tint would be more concentrated, and more reflective, because less of the available light is being reflected from the base material, and more of it is being reflected from the tint. I assume.

When it comes to animal rescue, I'd say stick to watching videos from places you know are reputable rescues - RSPCA, RSPB, shelters and conservation societies you recognise and know you can trust (Not PETA). Actually the potato hack works.  We often do it when our curries are overpowered with salt. We cut down some 1-2 potatoes and add it to the curry. We add more spices and water inorder to accumulate the potatoes. But we don't take the potatoes away. We will just eat it. It is also pretty tasty in curries with all the spices. But I think this only works if the potato goes well with the food we are preparing, like most of the Indian curries.
The potato hack is at least 100 year old but it ask for whole potato.

On the note of fake animal rescue: a LOT of videos of cats "dancing" and stuff like that are ones that have been drugged.   As a cat owner, i know cats do super weird things on their own, they make weird movements and flip upside down and springboard off of furniture.   But a dead giveaway is when theyre upright. The two videos that come to mind are the one of a cat "playing the piano" where its clearly been drugged/had its paws tied like a marionette doll. The other one is the super famous video of a cat standing upright with its arms out, "dancing" (hard to describe but my mom sees it on facebook all the time).   Ive seen less recently, but on "americas funniest home videos"/early internet viral videos, there were a lot of videos of cats that were obvipusly being filmed after being scared/chased by the owner. Not normal cat "zoomies", but like actual fear. One i remember was of a cat jumping out of a pool and trying to climb the wall because it was so terrified, and it had obviously been thrown into the pool just before they started videoing.   Anyway, rant over. It just really bothers me when people use animals as props. Especially when cats already do so many funny things on their own. And ESPECIALLY when people dont notice/care when they turn the videos into memes

‚ù§Ô∏è You are a legend, Ann. I love the idea of this mass experiment. What a powerful and fun opportunity. Thank you for calling out that the smallest differences can affect reproducibility. When I still worked in a lab, we discovered the reason I couldn't reproduce one of my labmate's results was because, after washing her glassware, she rinsed four times with DI H2O, while I rinsed the lab's standard of three times.
